[20/10/2025, 15:49:22] Eddie: So in the spirit of collaboration,  I need to pick the Product Brain Trust in this group. 
I am helping an organization scale ML Ope engineers. Two of the candidates I interviewed over MS Teams, had great/fantastic answers (timing+structure) Great right? 
Except there were pauses before every single answer and  the eyes kept moving from left to right as when you read. 
I am certain they were using AI to answer. 
As we say in America, _if it walks like a duck, and it quacks like duck, it must be a duck_ 

My question is, other
than brining them in-person what have you done or use to tackle the challenge of candidates using AI?
[20/10/2025, 15:53:55] ~ Martha Beith: The best thing I’ve seen people do is effectively make things ‘open book’. This removes the issue of whether they are using AI or not, and showing you how good they are at it. Particularly in engineers I found it shows you that they understand the fundamentals and can still check everything. ‎<This message was edited>
[20/10/2025, 15:54:47] ~ Martha Beith: They way we did it was to have a screen share and ask them what AI models/tools they used and why.
[20/10/2025, 15:55:06] ~ Martha Beith: Or if they eschewed them altogether.
[20/10/2025, 16:00:00] Eddie: Thank you, Martha. this is helpful.
[20/10/2025, 16:08:31] Gokul Raji: The other thing you could do is have an ambiguously broad question which forces them to take a stance and be able to reason well when on site. Keep enough time for Q & A as that shows how they think on the spot ‎<This message was edited>
[20/10/2025, 16:11:34] ~ Martin Röver-Parkes: I like to do a rapid-fire round covering product, process, and people questions. I frame this as a way to understand their ability to think on their feet, switch contexts, and respond quickly — it really shows whether they know their stuff without having time to rely on ChatGPT.

I also tell candidates they’re welcome to use AI tools during the interview if they want, but the questions will then be more challenging. If it turns into just reading off answers, I’ll call it out. It’s also a good way to see early on how they handle feedback and adapt in the moment.
[20/10/2025, 16:15:52] ~ Martha Beith: I would do that for product manager interviews, but for engineers I’m not always sure. Particularly because of personality types/skillsets, they are often the roles I find have the biggest deficit between being good verbal interviewees and doing good engineering work.

[27/10/2025, 09:01:19] ~ Scott: I've never seen one of those frameworks work 😆
They assume you can wipe the slate clean & start again, and have a singular mission across the organisation. The frameworks over- index on rigid structure. 
But most organisations aren't that neat, and are a mix of cultures and motivations. I think it's important to have a clear 'mindset' that makes intentions clear and let's us show up to complexity and ambiguity, then figure things out in a pragmatic way.
[27/10/2025, 09:06:57] ~ BMaz: Hi everyone I’m Brian - I lead product in financial services and love all things tech, AI, and great product design. Looking forward to getting to know you all.

https://www.linkedin.com/in/brianmazengera
[27/10/2025, 09:10:22] Natalia  🚀 Jaszczuk: Just to add, the way I've resolved to position the PMO is sort of as Chief of Staff function.. we have quite an immature product org, so PMO is there to ensure shit gets done, the right people are brought in at the right time, we're sticking to frameworks agreed etc.
Quite poss this should last us for the next 12 months - then we will see. BUT - ownership and accountability is defo an ongoing issue with PM. /PMO. ‎<This message was edited>

[11/11/2025, 17:12:10] ~ Martin Röver-Parkes: I’ve been part of some great peer groups in the Nordics, especially through Saasiest, and they were incredibly valuable. Each group was a proper commitment: one hour a month, and every session one member would present a key challenge or problem. Then the rest — seven or eight peers — would dive in together to unpack it. Loved it, made friends, mutual therapy, great networking. 

Soooo helpful to step outside my own bubble for a while, get fresh perspectives, and some much-needed reminders and refreshers!

The concept comes originally from military, emergency services, space, etc and is about the environment we work in being complex, uncertain and potentially dangerous - and how we adapt to these conditions. Some academics have expanded it to other forms of work like finance and healthcare, and I’m looking at it through a product and tech lens, as a foundation for my MSc in organisational psychology. 

I wrote a couple of articles on it already, most recent synthesising the systemic factors at play after a conversation in another product leader community. I’d love for folks to read it and share thoughts. I’m looking to dive into this more deeply so if you prefer you can DM me. 

https://open.substack.com/pub/liftoffwithcaroline/p/24-extreme-jobs-in-product-leadership
[11/11/2025, 21:13:03] Geoff Heddle: I have to say, you are researching several levels above where I am operating right now (somewhat intimidating) but my very gut reaction to your post is ‘What is extreme’? Female extreme could be Male “banter zone” etc.

[11/11/2025, 21:20:42] ~ Caroline Clark: Thanks Geoff - the first article explains what counts as an extreme jobs and I omitted that for brevity here:
- working 60+ hours per week
- ⁠unpredictable workflow
- ⁠always on availability
- ⁠tight deadlines and fast work pace
- ⁠scope of responsibility amounting to more than one job

There’s an element of subjectivity in how people respond to these factors for differing reasons, but I think most people would find this a challenging work context. There are a few who thrive on the adrenaline this brings!

[18/11/2025, 08:42:49] Anuj Gupta: 𝗧𝗵𝗲 𝗗𝗲𝘀𝗶𝗴𝗻 𝗣𝗮𝘁𝘁𝗲𝗿𝗻 𝗶𝗻 𝗖𝗼𝗻𝘃𝗲𝗿𝘀𝗮𝘁𝗶𝗼𝗻𝗮𝗹 𝗔𝗜 𝘁𝗵𝗮𝘁 𝗡𝗼𝗯𝗼𝗱𝘆 𝗧𝗮𝗹𝗸𝘀 𝗔𝗯𝗼𝘂𝘁: 𝗡𝗲𝗴𝗮𝘁𝗶𝘃𝗲 𝗖𝗼𝗻𝘃𝗲𝗿𝘀𝗮𝘁𝗶𝗼𝗻𝗮𝗹 𝗟𝗼𝗴𝗶𝗰

You’ve seen plenty of conversational voice AI systems that can answer specific questions/queries. Cute.

𝘉𝘶𝘵 𝘪𝘯 𝘵𝘩𝘦 𝘳𝘦𝘢𝘭 𝘸𝘰𝘳𝘭𝘥, 𝘨𝘳𝘦𝘢𝘵 𝘤𝘰𝘯𝘷𝘦𝘳𝘴𝘢𝘵𝘪𝘰𝘯𝘢𝘭 𝘈𝘐 𝘪𝘴𝘯’𝘵 𝘫𝘶𝘴𝘵 𝘢𝘣𝘰𝘶𝘵 𝘬𝘯𝘰𝘸𝘪𝘯𝘨 𝘸𝘩𝘢𝘵 𝘵𝘰 𝘴𝘢𝘺 — 𝘪𝘵’𝘴 𝘢𝘭𝘴𝘰 𝘢𝘣𝘰𝘶𝘵 𝘬𝘯𝘰𝘸𝘪𝘯𝘨 𝘸𝘩𝘢𝘵 𝘢𝘣𝘴𝘰𝘭𝘶𝘵𝘦𝘭𝘺 𝘕𝘖𝘛 𝘵𝘰 𝘴𝘢𝘺, 𝘦𝘷𝘦𝘯 𝘪𝘧 𝘴𝘰𝘮𝘦𝘰𝘯𝘦 𝘵𝘳𝘪𝘦𝘴 𝘦𝘷𝘦𝘳𝘺 𝘵𝘳𝘪𝘤𝘬 𝘪𝘯 𝘵𝘩𝘦 𝘣𝘰𝘰𝘬.

Because trust me, you don’t want a hospitality bot casually hurling abuses…
or a BFSI bot happily answering, “Sure sir, your neighbour's credit card number is...”.
or a bot for a European client that gives responses that violate GDPR! 

So how do you actually teach an AI “𝗧𝗵𝗼𝘂 𝘀𝗵𝗮𝗹𝘁 𝗡𝗢𝗧 𝘀𝗮𝘆 𝘁𝗵𝗶𝘀 — 𝘂𝗻𝗱𝗲𝗿 𝗔𝗡𝗬 𝗰𝗶𝗿𝗰𝘂𝗺𝘀𝘁𝗮𝗻𝗰𝗲𝘀”?
Everyone is talking about “𝘄𝗵𝗮𝘁 𝘁𝗼 𝗱𝗼.”
No one today is thinking about the “𝘄𝗵𝗮𝘁 𝗶𝘀 𝘀𝘁𝗿𝗶𝗰𝘁𝗹𝘆 𝗼𝗳𝗳-𝗹𝗶𝗺𝗶𝘁𝘀 𝗳𝗼𝗿 𝘆𝗼𝘂𝗿 𝗯𝘂𝘀𝗶𝗻𝗲𝘀𝘀 𝘂𝘀𝗲 𝗰𝗮𝘀𝗲”

I have been deep in this problem — building a conversational voice AI system that smooth-talks scammers for hours but never leaks a single piece of confidential info. Think of it as a polite talkative decoy that drains scammer energy like a boss.

[18/11/2025, 15:59:35] Max Mizzi: The most basic thing I tend to ask when introducing the idea of product is “who decided what to build?”
 
In this case the extension is “should the person deciding what to build report up to tech (so ultimately tech is deciding what to build) or to you (who currently decides what to build and is looking to delegate that practice — practice of deciding what to build, not decision making authority)

(Side note: most first product hires report to CTOs, but it really varies based on who the people are and the way roles are cut at such a early stage) ‎<This message was edited>
[18/11/2025, 16:01:38] Nick Whitford: I'm surprised where they sit in the org at an early stage startup is all that important anyway. * the overlap will exist regardless, it's just whether it's direct or indirect
* having someone with a similar breadth of context to soundboard with directly would be useful
* if you soundboard with them a lot but they report to tech, that could make the tech person a bit nervous
* most experienced product people I know would be comfortable helping the CEO get comfortable with the overlap - IME it's pretty common even with more seasoned CEOs.

Just my 10c, obvs 😊 ‎<This message was edited>

[19/11/2025, 12:15:23] ~ Sascha Brossmann: I’ve been in that role several times. It’s a challenging stretch, but an interesting one with massive impact 🙂 Here’s what often gets overlooked: 
- ⁠The first senior product hire comes with an eject seat attached to their bottoms. Part of the job is to figure out what kind of product leader the organisation _actually_ needs. Ideally it fits you but not necessarily so. Both the founders and the potential hire should be aware of that. Also, the “clear roles and boundaries” mentioned above are NOT clear upon arrival. You have to actively figure them out. It helps a lot to get a clear mandate from the founders, though (my last one was roughly: “get us to PMF and enable us to scale, change whatever is necessary for that purpose, we’re happy to learn”)
- You need to be damn good at thinking and acting cross-functionally, way beyond product. This is a many-hats role with _lots_ of context switching. I remember weeks where I was pitching to investors as the founder’s sidekick, working on high-level strategy, updating budgets, interviewing customers, creating marketing slide decks, writing web site and app store copy, sketching user flows and features, ordering food for the devs before a launch, and emptying the office bins. Early stage startup is an all-hands-on-deck environment with very fluid roles. 
- ⁠The impact of the first hire cannot be understated. They will have to model what “good” looks like in their domain. They will also have to coach and mentor not only the product team but the whole fledgling company, especially the leadership team. They will have to establish standards, ways of working, lightweight processes and governance, in short: they will have to lay the foundation for building the whole operating system. I would hence strongly recommend _against_ hiring someone with a pure IC background. Or someone who has only experience with more mature/evolved post-PMF company environments. Pre-PMF product is a special kind of beast with totally different playbooks (and much less canonical advice out there to google). Alternative: get a good senior IC with zero-to-one experience paired with a fractional CPO or coach/advisor (shameless plug: I’m currently available :))
[19/11/2025, 12:17:47] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Designers in early stage startup are often too junior to do more upstream work. Had to coach my last one _extensively_.
[19/11/2025, 12:29:57] ~ Sascha Brossmann: I’d second that. I consider my own design background (two decades ago) a major success factor in my early stage product leadership roles (not just for UX – facilitation, making ideas visible/tangible, and sensemaking are also important aspects). I also had extensive business/commercial exposure, though. Financial literacy is crucial. Someone in that role needs to be able to operate with a C-level perspective and understand the financial model or a term sheet as much as the user journey.
[19/11/2025, 12:38:40] ~ Sascha Brossmann: An experienced scale-up VP will most likely bring the wrong playbook unless they also had early-stage exposure. I’d eventually look for one of these later (somewhere between 50-100 headcount) in case the early-stage product leader struggles with scaling the system.

[24/11/2025, 18:50:54] ~ Sascha Brossmann: +1 to @⁨~Mikkel Nielsen⁩ – I also _do_ think that there's a distinction, especially when you look beyond the small subset of AI that LLMs are. Essentially, AI is _platform_ and not frontline technology. Do we agree that platform and frontline products require different flavours of PM?

Also, the probabilistic/non-deterministic nature of LLMs and other models requires a clearly distinct approach to evaluating your bets. It's much harder to tell with confidence if something is moving the needle as expected when outcomes can vary _a lot_ with context. Add a massive load of quite particular ITsec considerations, and altogether far more than usual technical understanding and you get a distinct profile. As you might have guessed by now, I currently assume that the AI PM profile merges into the platform PM profile. 

As for AI tooling for the majority of PM work, that's a different discussion.

[26/11/2025, 14:01:47] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Love it! Good framework, more actionable than my default advice of using Minto’s _pyramid principle_ for exec comms. 

Caveat: in order to use your framework, you need a working model that connects product KPIs with business impact. As doubleloop’s Daniel Schmidt calls it: a _value architecture_ (see https://blog.doubleloop.app/value-architecture/ – excellent article IMHO). 

I often find communication issues caused more by lack of clarity than by lack of skill, which in turn frequently results from underdeveloped modelling practices, commonly paired with an equally deficient measuring capability (Hubbard’s “How to Measure Anything” is frequently amongst the first textbooks I tend to throw both at teams and executives ;)). At the root this is a systemic problem. Which also makes it a clear senior leadership responsibility.

[01/12/2025, 15:16:22] ~ Sascha Brossmann: One of the best articles about organisational development and structure I’ve read in _quite_ a while: https://synexia.substack.com/p/the-hierarchy-of-work-is-inescapable I’d be surprised if you would not get at least one valuable novel idea from reading. No matter whether you prefer to handle complexity via managerial accountability hierarchy or via self-organisation. 

Excerpt:

> Every organisation, from a hospital to a bakery to a national intelligence agency, must handle, at minimum, four categories of complexity. These aren’t abstract categories; they are the four unavoidable loads that every organisation must assign to someone or something.
> Once you understand that complexity must be carried somewhere, the next question is where it sits in the organisation.
> (…)
> Work complexity is still stratified, it simply has different carriers.
> This matters because far too many organisations read Buurtzorg as a story about culture, empowerment, or mindset. It is actually a story about the mechanisms you choose, where you locate them, and how you architect the system so the unavoidable work complexity has someplace rational to live.
> And that is the point that both Jaquesians and anti-hierarchists routinely overlook.
> It is a story about the mechanisms you use to absorb complexity, which ones you choose, where you locate them, and how you architect the systems so the unavoidable work complexity has rational places to live.
> Complexity must always be matched with complexity. If you don’t design deliberate mechanisms to absorb it, it will simply reappear somewhere else, usually in the form of overload, escalation, or accidental hierarchy.

At the same time, resolving aforementioned misaligned incentives often gets escalated to senior leadership calling the shots on portfolio budgets instead of enabling teams to resolve them autonomously, faster, and more effectively via operating system evolution.

[08/12/2025, 23:03:10] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Thanks for sharing, Izabella! Here are my reading notes: 

> product leaders must impact P&L, margins, and value pools — not just “speak finance.”

I'd argue that this was always the case. Just not broadly understood. AI changed nothing about it. But *post-ZIRP economics* did! (I attribute several significant shifts you're touching to the change in macro economics rather than to Gen AI crossing the mass product threshold.)

> AI collapses boundaries between product, commercial, and technical decisions;

How so? What's the major difference to a competent cross-functional team?

> Founder-level ownership means shaping pricing, influencing cost structure, prioritising value pools, and making the commercial trade-offs that determine whether a product scales or quietly sunsets.

Hell, yes! But this was already important. Just not common (which I always considered weird).

> PM role convergence is here

Different perspective: the one role that's _collapsing_ is the backlog-managing tactical PM with limited scope and little commercial impact. You don't need a PM in every product team anymore. A more senior PM (or GM!) role further upstream with a _commercial_ focus is probably enough. The tactical PM role gets merged into the other roles.

At the same time I expect to see more functions (growth, revenue, CS, data/research/BI, legal, compliance, …) directly represented in product teams => end-to-end value stream ownership.

> A single senior IC or AI-native builder can deliver what once required PM × Data × Design × Eng Leads.

No, they cannot. Unless you're aiming for non-differentiating product quality on the lower end of mediocrity. “Good” needs expert judgment. You underestimate the vast gap between what people without expertise can do with AI now and what experts can do. Competent judgment in domains like design and engineering requires a specific kind of expertise built through years of reflective practice. No shortcuts there. Gen AI tools without expert guidance are not getting even remotely close to that. 

Besides, while smaller teams can achieve more than ever before, you also want a) sufficient cognitive diversity for quality and b) robustness (less people => higher bus factor – resilient teams and systems require redundancies to accommodate for disruptive risks). 

> Viability becomes the filter: *Does this change ARR, LTV, margin, or cost? If not, why do it? If yes, which metric matters most now?*

Why "becomes" and not "is still"?

> Within this context, systems thinking is the discipline of designing product lines, portfolio evolution, and user-interaction shifts as **one interconnected system** — where architecture, behaviour, and business performance influence each other.
> 
> The North Star becomes **modular architecture**, l**ayered value propositions**, and **clarity on who** you serve **and what** problem you solve, rather than the convenient solution that retrofits into legacy structures, assumes homogeneous customer behaviour, or preserves outdated commercial models — **solutions that inevitably require sunsetting within 12–24 months.**
>
> A systems approach enables products to evolve with customer behaviour, technology, **business and monetisation models**, and competition through feedback loops and dynamic adaptation, avoiding the need for painful rebuilds every cycle.

Emphatic yes!

> Generative UX

Yes! Perhaps the most underindexed viable use case for LLMs in digital products.

> We are moving from segmentation to individualisation; traditional interface norms will not hold for long.

Caveat: individualisation is _hard_ because fundamental UI principles related to ergonomics, cognitive load, etc. persist.

> Sound judgment now makes or breaks product leadership. AI Execution is faster, cheaper, and easier, but the **consequences of poor choices arrive sooner**.

Yes!

> AI can generate prototypes instantly

1. expert teams could do the same nearly as fast already decades ago. It's just a matter of skill paired with a good component library. 
2. Not all prototypes are the same. AI tools can only generate a subset of useful prototypes. 
3. Knowing what and how to protype > moderate prototyping speed gains. 

> competitive advantage no longer comes from output speed but from *discernment* — the ability to judge what is viable, what is worth doing and why VS what is noise

"no longer"? When and where was output speed an actual competitive advantage?

> In a high-velocity, high-uncertainty environment, sound judgment becomes the only stable differentiator. It enables leaders to make clear decisions, avoid false signals, and align teams around a direction that compounds value rather than waste.

Emphatic yes. At the same time: no change in that respect. That was already the case for a long time. ‎<This message was edited>

[10/12/2025, 13:08:29] Natalia  🚀 Jaszczuk: one of my lightbulb moments was learning about Conway's law. Original: 	“Organizations design systems that mirror their own communication structures.” It can be adapted to basically mean: The way you organise people determines what work gets done, how decisions are made, and ultimately what value the organisation creates.
I used it mostly to justify why certain functions should be on LT, and how the layer below them and the matrix, ultimately impacts what gets prioritised ‎<This message was edited>

[15/12/2025, 08:02:03] Erik Schwartz: Two tips for getting Claude Code *not* to implement things right away. 

1. Use planning mode. Shift-tab puts CC into planning mode where it will ask questions about a larger change. 
2. ⁠if you are particularly stuck on a bug, tell it to ultrathink- literally use the term ultrathink in your prompt and it will shift into thinking mode and use maximum tokens to work out a solution. 

I leave thinking mode on by default particularly when I’m building.  It’s also helpful to say, don’t make any changes right now but act as a system architect or a DBA and think through the best solution. 

If you still find Claude is too eager and builds too quickly try Codex - I find openai much less eager on the build side, which is why I don’t use it in my workshops for non coders, or do your thinking outside of Claude Code like in ChatGPT or Gemini.
[15/12/2025, 08:03:12] ~ Esin: Hi Moreno, welcome to London. There will be a meetup in Jan https://luma.com/w4y19dpc
[15/12/2025, 08:03:39] ~ Rafael Franco (Raf): Cursor has these 3 modes incorporated: agent, ask and plan
[15/12/2025, 08:09:54] Erik Schwartz: Very true. You just have to remember to click on the ui to change the mode. It won’t change the modes directly from a prompt. 

It’s amazing how dependent we have become on a mouse to point and click. 

But at the end of the day what matters most is to find the tool that suits you best.  I can completely see why for many a CLI like Claude Code or Codex can feel too much like a black box.

[17/12/2025, 09:54:19] ~ Andrew Towers: Hi everyone - I’ve recently joined the community and it’s great to see how active and supportive it is 👏🏽

In my previous workplace I wanted to implement a tool to reduce overhead and provide a more robust “single source of truth” on product goals, priorities and yes, roadmaps, for our various squads.  Our delivery workflow was pretty good (Jira) and after assessing a handful of tools (including Productboard) we went with Craft.io.  We selected it in part as we thought it did a stronger job of consolidating the discovery side of Product and was flexible to our needs.  Other tools seemed to overlap more with Jira, which we didn’t need.

In reality, everywhere I have worked the exec comms is on powerpoint / slides with the occasional workshop 🤷‍♂️

https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Obeya
[17/12/2025, 10:39:04] ~ Sascha Brossmann: A propos: as for transparency, alignment, connecting work at different org levels etc. I consider this more a domain of structure, process, and culture rather than tooling. Current favourite approach (used in all recent companies): Klaus Leopold’s agile Flight Levels. Lightweight, accessible, adaptable – like Kanban it works everywhere. Decent intro: https://mattphilip.wordpress.com/2023/03/21/introduction-to-flight-levels/ 

The two biggest benefits in my experience: 
1. providing structural support for clarifying how outcome-focused work flows though different levels of the system while staying agnostic about implementation etc.
2. ⁠introducing a value stream coordination layer between roadmap and operational work. Game changer in all environments I’ve witnessed it. Substitutes a lot of common alignment meetings & co. while keeping the conversation focused. 

I could host an intro/AMA session next year if you are interested.

[08/01/2026, 19:14:51] ~ Rebecca: Did you come to a conclusion on where you want to publish ?
[08/01/2026, 20:14:51] ~ Sascha Brossmann: I’m still on the fence between Ghost and Beehiiv, primarily because I haven’t clarified my content strategy, yet. Ghost is suited more for blogging-style publication while the open source foundation makes it long-term risk free in terms of ownership etc., Beehiiv is better for email newsletters and offers better audience growth support including segmentation and tracking. Drawback: little automation support AFAIK. For newsletters, Bento (https://bentonow.com/) also looks interesting (technically probably the most powerful platform, with outstanding automation support), though it’s more of a general marketing and less a focused publishing solution.

[11/01/2026, 17:07:04] ~ Caroline Clark: Hello all, happy Sunday! I’m working on the core premise for my first book and testing which idea resonates most - would appreciate your initial thoughts 🙂 

*Which of these best names something you’ve personally experienced, but haven’t had good language for?*

1.⁠ ⁠Beliefs as Operating Systems
Beliefs aren’t opinions or self-talk. They’re the hidden operating system that shapes how we interpret information, make decisions, and act under pressure.

2.⁠ ⁠Training for the Unknown
Modern work increasingly involves uncertainty, incomplete rules, and noisy feedback, yet we’re rarely trained for those conditions.

3.⁠ ⁠Capability Breakdowns
Many performance issues aren’t skill gaps, but breakdowns in how capable people interpret situations, assess risk, and trust their own judgement as context changes.

I’ll add a poll below to make it easy to vote!

[13/01/2026, 09:22:49] Jason Knight: personal opinion as someone who has been grinding the content mill for a while now: It’s oversaturated, filled with AI slop and harder to stand out than ever. I don’t believe you’ll get meaningful “better opportunities” out of just publishing stuff.

That said, I think you should do it anyway, because at the very least it will help you sharpen your thinking/get your story straight; this has second order effects where you will feel and sound more confident because you’re not making stuff up on the fly. Also, the world needs more diversity of perspective and, even if you only get a handful of people reading, you can still make meaningful change to them

[13/01/2026, 15:14:33] ~ Scott: @⁨~Carla⁩ hey Carla -- in my head I've got two types of writing:

-- field guides: I've been doing the above since 2010, and have used them to come up with my own models and approaches. These I turn into guides for other people that I publish through my newsletter. This is much more for professional profile and networking. ‎<This message was edited>

[14/01/2026, 08:05:57] ~ Igor: Good morning, 

I’m exploring an podcast idea with Aral, a senior podcast producer, and would love a quick sense-check from my network.

There’s no shortage of AI podcasts — but most focus on trends, breakthroughs, or research. What feels missing is clear, tactical guidance on actually implementing AI in product and tech teams.

The concept we’re discussing:
* A video + audio podcast for product and technology leaders with AI scar tissues, not consultants 
* Real stories on integrating and operationalising AI
* What worked, what didn’t, and where teams wasted serious time or money
* Less hype, more data topics, guardrails, security, ROI

It’s aimed at people building roadmaps, managing AI features, and trying not to burn £/$500k on tools that never make it to production.

If something like this existed:
* Would you listen?

[16/01/2026, 10:05:26] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Why not let the team members map their skills? I’d probably start with mapping the required capabilities for the team instead. My favourite approach comes from Chris McDermott: https://medium.com/@chrisvmcd/mapping-maturity-create-context-specific-maturity-models-with-wardley-maps-informed-by-cynefin-37ffcd1d315 ‎<This message was edited>
[16/01/2026, 10:11:30] ~ Niek: A great! I was searching for such a book
[16/01/2026, 10:12:04] ~ Caroline Clark: I’ve done it a few times for Product and design too, but not for lead dev. Do you have an eng manager or design lead you could lean on here?

The heuristic I use is to split skills / knowledge into 3 buckets:
1. Human (soft) skills - these can be standardised across all 3, what differs is application and context
2. ⁠Technical (hard) skills - what is role-specific?
3. ⁠Domain specific skills - what do they need to know about your particular context, e.g. healthtech -> regulation, compliance

A mind map might help here to get it all out on paper first. Then I would use Dreyfus model of skill acquisition to determine the level required for each role from entry through to lead.
[16/01/2026, 10:13:04] ~ Caroline Clark: I’ve got a map for the human skills, which I can share in a DM
[16/01/2026, 10:18:58] ~ Lisa: Petra Wille’s is amazing
[16/01/2026, 10:19:47] ~ Lisa: https://www.petra-wille.com/blog/category/PMwheel
[16/01/2026, 10:20:46] ~ Sascha Brossmann: I’d still anchor all these to the team’s desired capabilities. In the end it also does not matter _who_ in a team provides the skills for these, you just need _someone_. So you don’t need to be strict about associating the skills to specific functional roles. I’m also a big fan of decoupling roles from jobs/positions. Roles can be shifted around then. You get much better learning and development opportunities for your people that way.
[16/01/2026, 10:32:39] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Here’s a nice intro TL;DR by Andreas Holmer on role-based organisation: https://medium.com/workmatters/org-lego-responsibilities-roles-and-positions-8c784229935a

[21/01/2026, 22:32:35] ~ Robin: Someone already said it, but I’d definitely suggest digging into what didn’t work with the previous candidates (getting really deep into the details from multiple people) and then use typical user insights grouping into theme or categories to form a holistic picture. Figure out what “poor fit” looks like and use that to craft what good looks like.

Not an exhaustive list, but some of the common ones I’ve seen:

Lack of agreement on strategy/priority
Not being able to effectively collaborate/communicate
Mismatch of skills to needs of company at the time
Mismatch of culture
Under-delivering and/or unrealistic expectations
[21/01/2026, 22:37:41] ~ Hannah Day: Interviews are such a false process as well. Communication, as said above, is so critical at this level, so how do we assess that? I almost always have to ask to meet my Engineering partner as they are not included in the interview, and if these people can’t communicate well, there are problems. Then the people and leadership side. Yes, this person is very strategic and visionary, but can the lead and motivate. Can they bring people on a journey. How can you see this better at interview.
[21/01/2026, 23:00:05] ~ Michelle Wright: I’ve spent a lot of time thinking about this lately, having left my role after just under 2 years. I think this is particularly relevant for a company’s first CPO.

The brief is usually: transform how we build product and keep delivery moving. In practice, transformation is an influence-led change effort that takes time, political capital, and visible executive sponsorship - which may not be there. Delivery, meanwhile, demands certainty, speed, and short-term results - which also may need an overhaul. Those two forces actively work against each other in the first 12–18 months.

A few hard truths I have seen:
	•	Transformation without delivery credibility fails
If the first CPO does not stabilise delivery quickly, they lose trust and influence before transformation has a chance to land.
	•	Delivery without transformation just burns the CPO out
If all energy goes into shipping under existing constraints, the organisation never changes, and the role becomes a senior project manager with an expiry date.
	•	Influence is the real job, but rarely acknowledged
As a first CPO, most of the work is not artefacts or roadmaps. It is decision hygiene, priority trade-offs, educating the exec team, and resetting expectations. That work is invisible, slow, and uncomfortable, yet essential.
	•	Time horizons are misaligned
Boards and CEOs often expect material transformation in 6–9 months. That is not realistic unless the organisation is already product-leaning. Without patience at the top, failure is predictable.

In my opinion if companies want a first CPO to succeed, they have to make an explicit choice:
either stabilise delivery first and accept slower transformation,
or back true transformation and protect the role from short-term delivery noise. 
Happy to chat more from my lived experience. 🥴
[22/01/2026, 07:11:01] ~ Sascha Brossmann: A lot of this boils down to the critical distinction between working _in_ the system and working _on_ the system. Plus the current understanding and level of respective awareness in the org leadership.
[22/01/2026, 07:12:35] ~ Sascha Brossmann: Situational awareness at the exec level is also key. Especially when hiring for their first product peer.
[22/01/2026, 07:12:46] Jessie: All of this is so valid, thanks for all your input. 
I’ve been helping to hire in product for 15 years and the expectations, remit, approach, understanding, mandate for these roles, especially leadership vary so widely. Also some hiring managers keep recruiters at arms length, even when it’s a strategic hire the briefs can be dreadful and they don’t want to invest the time in proper qualification, not allowing for deep questions. 
The good ones, do take time and are prepared to talk/listen and be flexible and value insight and feedback. 
This investment upfront  and collaboration makes it much more of a partnership and you get a much better shortlist of candidates and a much better experience all round. This is the way we like to work but it doesn’t always happen. 
I do push back and I never work just off spec but it’s amazing the number of clients who give a spec and expect you to find them their perfect fit ( when they don’t often know what they want!) 
Can make things tricky and waste a lot of people’s time and effort. ‎<This message was edited>
[22/01/2026, 07:23:58] Nick Whitford: Very very very well said.
The point in time frames for transformation is bang on. And the kicker is that even if you miraculously transform something substantial about the org, it might take another 3-9 months to see a commercial impact from it, purely because revenue is such a lagging indicator
[22/01/2026, 08:42:13] Nick Whitford: This topic has really got me wondering if the ambition of Product leaders is our double-edged sword.
@⁨~Michelle Wright⁩'s comments are so clear about the common challenges that would often sit under two C-level heads...
A Chief Transformation Officer (or equivalent) and the CPO.
Makes me wonder if what's really needed is a right-hand-person who leads the transformation effort in tandem with the day-to-day Product work (or some other solution) rather than bearing the weight of that dual-responsibility. 
Transformations of any kind are more likely to fail than succeed - I wrote an article on them a while ago and opened with this eye-opening stat:

Research from Bain, BCG, McKinsey, and other major consultancies shows that the average success rate of transformations is just 25%, with Bain reporting a tragically low 12% success rate. According to the large consulting firms, the top 5 reasons for failure are:

Leadership Alignment and Sponsorship (McKinsey, Bain, BCG)
Engaging the Workforce (McKinsey, Deloitte, Bain)
Comprehensive Change Management (McKinsey, BCG, Deloitte)
Realistic and Achievable Goals (BCG, Bain, McKinsey)
Adequate Resources and Capabilities (Deloitte, BCG, McKinsey)
[22/01/2026, 08:52:36] ~ Caroline Clark: To add thoughts around how hard transformation is, I wrote this article earlier in the week and shared it in the Leadership group: https://liftoffwithcaroline.substack.com/p/30-why-organisational-transformations

It IS a tall order for one person to do alone, you need all your leadership pulling in the same direction, believing the same thing. It’s not just about goals or strategies or metrics, it’s what’s under the hood, what’s going on in everybody’s minds. There can be surface level agreement that something needs to happen, and private reservations and cognitive dissonance that means groups don’t move in the same direction together.

in my experience I’ve seen product transformations fail or stall because on paper people can see how it could work, but in practice don’t have the ability to wait for the results, e.g. need to land funding, need to hit budgets, etc. Different teams incentivised in different ways (I go into one common clash between sales and product in the article).

It’s not fair to bring a single product leader into an organisation and expect them to work miracles in changing how they work and get better results, without also being willing to do the work themselves (looking at CEOs, c-suite teams, founders).
[22/01/2026, 08:57:29] ~ Mark: There is a really tight link-up here between the tech eco-system and the org expectations. You will find that the org wants 'transformation' but they want it in the current delivery constraints - 'we want all the payment options' - but then don't want to go on an 18 month journey to change PSPs or re-calibrate PCI compliance on the front end. I have found that presenting these as optionalities helps with the conversation, but there still needs to be appetite to recognise that change might mean really fundamental shifts, in tech, people, culture, and teams, and that those need to be explicitly leaned into.

[23/01/2026, 17:46:56] ~ Ashwin: See this <object at hand>? 
Someone decided it should exist, what it costs to make, what price to sell, what colours should it come and how big it should be - to make money.

I used to do that when building hardware/physical products. 

Now I do the same thing for software. 
Like Netflix - someone decided - 'Let's charge 15 quid a month. Let's spend 100000 building a download feature. Will enough people pay for it to make that worth it?'
I make those calls for X  - what to build, what it'll cost us, and whether we'll make money from it, and then ensure the work gets completed.
Caveats abound. Key has always been to use a relatable example. ‎<This message was edited>
